Idraj in the Tashahhud Hadith and Its Fiqh Impact
Analysis of idraj in the tashahhud hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the agreement of hadith scholars on idraj in the tashahhud hadith impacts the Hanafi ruling on optionality after tashahhud.

Core Idea
When authentic hadith scholars unanimously identify an addition as mudraj, it cannot be used as prophetic evidence.

The tashahhud hadith includes an addition that suggests optionality after completing the testimony. However, leading hadith scholars have confirmed this addition is an insertion from Ibn Mas'ud's own words. This lesson explores how this affects the Hanafi ruling on post-tashahhud actions.

Guided Path
1
State the addition in the tashahhud hadith
The addition says: 'When you have said this, you have completed your prayer; if you wish to stand, stand; if you wish to sit, sit.'
This appears at the end of the hadith.
2
Identify the narrator who inserted it
Zuhayr bin Mu'awiya is the narrator who inserted the addition from his own words.
He inserted the statement of Ibn Mas'ud into the hadith.
3
Note the consensus of hadith scholars on idraj
Al-Daraqutni, al-Hakim, al-Bayhaqi, al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, and others agreed the addition is mudraj.
Their agreement strengthens the conclusion.
4
Recognize the evidence supporting idraj
Ibn al-Salah provides evidence: the trustworthy Abd al-Rahman bin Thabit narrated the hadith without the addition.
This contradition confirms the addition is extra.
5
Apply the Hanafi usul principle
An addition proven to be mudraj is not considered prophetic, so it does not establish a ruling.
Thus, the optionality after tashahhud is not from the sunnah.

Chapter Three / Disagreement in Chain and Text. And Muhammad bin Dinar is not one whose memory is relied upon. And Hisham bin Hassan narrated from Hisham bin Urwa from his father from Busra as an insertion (mudraj). The conclusion of the research was the weakness of the first follow-up and the lack of precision of the second. And this hadith had an impact on the disagreement of jurists, which was discussed earlier in Chapter Two, Section Three: on matters of general affliction; we do not wish to repeat it to avoid length. And an example of insertion occurring at the end of the hadith: what Zuhayr bin Mu'awiya narrated from al-Hasan bin al-Hurr from al-Qasim bin Mukhaymira from Alqama from Abdullah bin Mas'ud that the Messenger of Allah taught him the tashahhud in prayer, saying: "Say: 'Greetings to Allah...' and he mentioned the hadith." And at its end: "I bear witness that there is no god but Allah and I bear witness that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah. When you have said this, you have completed your prayer; if you wish to stand, stand, and if you wish to sit, sit." The addition "When you have said this... to the end of the narration" is inserted (mudraj) from the statement of Ibn Mas'ud; Zuhayr bin Mu'awiya inserted it into his narration from al-Hasan bin al-Hurr. This was stated by the hadith scholars including: al-Daraqutni, al-Hakim, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, and it was reported from al-Nawawi in "al-Khulasa" that the scholars agreed upon its insertion. And Ibn al-Salah argued for the insertion by saying: "Evidence for it is that the trustworthy ascetic Abd al-Rahman bin Thabit bin Thuban narrated it from his narrator al-Hasan bin al-Hurr in the same way, and they agreed."
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is from the third chapter on disagreement in chain and text, focusing on the phenomenon of idraj (insertion) in hadith. The author provides two examples to illustrate how idraj affects fiqh differences. First, the hadith about touching the private parts: the narrator Hisham bin Hassan is criticized for lack of precision; his wording fluctuates, indicating weak memory. Some narrators added the part about testicles, which led to juristic disagreement on whether touching the private parts invalidates wudu. The author notes that this was covered earlier. Second, the tashahhud hadith: the addition at the end permitting sitting or standing after testifying was inserted by Zuhayr bin Mu'awiya from the statement of Ibn Mas'ud, not from the Prophet. Scholars like al-Daraqutni, al-Hakim, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Khatib al-Baghdadi confirmed the idraj. Ibn al-Salah provides evidence that another narrator transmitted without the addition. Understanding idraj is crucial for Hanafi usul al-fiqh because it affects the authenticity of evidence used in deriving rulings.

Key Terms
إدراج (idraj): Insertion of a narrator's own statement into the hadith text, making it appear as part of the prophetic wording
مدرج (mudraj): A hadith with an insertion; the added part is from a narrator, not the Prophet

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because Zuhayr bin Mu'awiya was a weak narrator
B) Because the addition is absent in other authentic narrations and scholars like al-Daraqutni identified it as Ibn Mas'ud's statement
C) Because the addition contradicts the Quran
D) Because the addition was about wudu, not tashahhud

Key Takeaway
Hadith scholars' consensus on idraj overrides individual narrations and guides fiqh rulings.

Idraj in Hadith: The Tashahhud Addition of Ibn Mas'ud
textual analysis of idraj in tashahhud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a case of idraj in a hadith by comparing chains and applying scholarly consensus on insertion.

Core Idea
Idraj: a narrator's words inserted into prophetic text – the tashahhud addition is a classic example resolved through chain analysis.

This lesson examines a famous hadith on the tashahhud, where an extra phrase appears at the end. The question: is it part of the Prophet's teaching or Ibn Mas'ud's own explanation? By analyzing chains (sanad) and the statements of hadith masters, we see how the Hanafi usul distinguishes prophetic from non-prophetic speech. Understanding idraj is essential for correct istinbat (derivation of rulings).

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed addition
Locate the phrase that is claimed to be idraj. In the tashahhud hadith, the extra words are: 'When you have said this...' The base tashahhud ends before that.
The disputed text is 'فإذا قلت هذا فقد قضيت صلاتك إن شئت أن تقوم فقم، وإن شئت أن تقعد فاقعد'.
2
Survey the chains (sanad)
Collect all available narrations of this hadith. Note which narrators include the phrase and which omit it. The author lists narrators like Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah (included), versus Husayn al-Ju'fi and Ibn 'Ajlan (omitted).
Focus on the chain from al-Hasan ibn al-Hurr to Ibn Mas'ud.
3
Apply the criteria for idraj
Hadith scholars use evidence such as: a reliable narrator explicitly separating the phrase and attributing it to the companion, multiple narrators omitting it, and consensus of huffaz. Here, Abd al-Rahman ibn Thabit ibn Thawban narrated it with separation, and others omitted it.
The agreement of scholars on idraj is a strong indicator.
4
Derive the usul principle
From this analysis, the Hanafi usul principle emerges: a phrase that appears in some chains but is proven to be from a narrator's own words is not part of the prophetic sunna. It cannot be used as a dalil for a ruling.
This affects fiqh: the permissibility to stand or sit after tashahhud is not from the Prophet.

الفصل الثالث / الاختلاف في السند والمتن
مخيمرة عن علقمة، عن عبد الله بن مسعود أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم علمه التشهد في الصلاة، فقال: «قل: التحيات الله.. فذكر الحديث». وفي آخره: «أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وأشهد أن محمداً رسول الله، فإذا قلت هذا فقد قضيت صلاتك إن شئت أن تقوم فقم، وإن شئت أن تقعد فاقعد». فزيادة: «فإذا قلت هذا ... إلى نهاية الرواية مدرجة من قول ابن مسعود، أدرجها زهير بن معاوية في روايته عن الحسن بن الحر، نص على هذا الحفاظ منهم: الدارقطني، والحاكم، والبيهقي، والخطيب البغدادي، ونقل النووي في " الخلاصة " اتفاق الحفاظ على إدراجها. واستدل الحافظ ابن الصلاح على الإدراج بقوله: (ومن الدليل عليه أن الثقة الزاهد عبد الرحمن بن ثابت بن ثوبان، رواه عن راويه الحسن بن الحر كذلك، واتفق حسين الجعفي وابن عجلان وغيرهما في روايتهم عن الحسن بن الحر على ترك ذكر هذا الكلام في آخر الحديث مع اتفاق كل من روى التشهد عن علقمة – وعن غيره – عن ابن مسعود على ذلك، ورواه شبابة، عن أبي خيثمة ففصله أيضاً). وهذا كلام مجمل بيانه فيما يأتي: أولاً: رواه عبد الرحمن بن ثابت بن ثوبان عن الحسن بن الحر، بسند زهير بن معاوية، وفصل نهاية الرواية وبين أنها من قول ابن مسعود، وروايته عند ابن حبان، والطبراني، والدارقطني، والحاكم، والبيهقي، والخطيب البغدادي. ثانياً: رواه حسين الجعفي وابن عجلان واتفقا على عدم ذكر هذا الكلام في نهاية الرواية. ورواية حسين أخرجها ابن أبي شيبة، وأحمد، وابن حبان، والطبراني، والدارقطني، والخطيب. وأما رواية ابن عجلان فأخرجها الطبراني، والدارقطني، والخطيب.
Chapter Three / Difference in Isnad and Matn. Mikhmarah from 'Alqamah, from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud that the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) taught him the tashahhud in prayer, saying: 'Say: At-tahiyyatu lillah...' and he mentioned the hadith. And at its end: 'I bear witness that there is no god but Allah and I bear witness that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah. When you have said this, you have completed your prayer. If you wish to stand, stand; if you wish to sit, sit.' So the addition: 'When you have said this...' to the end of the narration is inserted (idraj) from the words of Ibn Mas'ud. Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah inserted it in his narration from al-Hasan ibn al-Hurr. The hadith scholars explicitly stated this, among them: al-Daraqutni, al-Hakim, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Khatib al-Baghdadi. Al-Nawawi in 'al-Khulasah' reported the agreement of the scholars on its insertion. Al-Hafiz Ibn al-Salah adduced evidence for the insertion by saying: 'Among the evidence for it is that the trustworthy ascetic 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Thabit ibn Thawban narrated it from his narrator al-Hasan ibn al-Hurr in the same way, and Husayn al-Ju'fi, Ibn 'Ajlan, and others agreed in their narration from al-Hasan ibn al-Hurr to omit mentioning this speech at the end of the hadith, while all who narrated the tashahhud from 'Alqamah and from others from Ibn Mas'ud agreed upon that, and Shababah narrated it from Abu Khaythamah and separated it as well.' This is a summary; its explanation follows: First: 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Thabit ibn Thawban narrated it from al-Hasan ibn al-Hurr via the chain of Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah, and he separated the end of the narration and clarified that it is from the speech of Ibn Mas'ud. His narration is found with Ibn Hibban, al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, al-Hakim, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Khatib al-Baghdadi. Second: Husayn al-Ju'fi and Ibn 'Ajlan narrated it and both agreed not to mention this speech at the end of the narration. Husayn's narration was recorded by Ibn Abi Shaybah, Ahmad, Ibn Hibban, al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, and al-Khatib. As for Ibn 'Ajlan's narration, it was recorded by al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, and al-Khatib.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a detailed textual analysis of a hadith on the tashahhud (the testimony recited during prayer). The author presents the narration from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud that the Prophet taught him the tashahhud, including an extra phrase at the end allowing the worshipper to stand or sit after completing the tashahhud. The central issue is whether this extra phrase is part of the Prophet's words or an insertion (idraj) by Ibn Mas'ud himself. The author establishes that the majority of hadith scholars consider this phrase to be inserted from Ibn Mas'ud's own explanation, not part of the prophetic text. Evidence is given: (1) A reliable narrator, Abd al-Rahman ibn Thabit ibn Thawban, narrated it separately and clarified it was Ibn Mas'ud's statement. (2) Other narrators like Husayn al-Ju'fi and Ibn 'Ajlan narrated the same hadith without this addition. (3) All those who narrated the tashahhud from Alqamah and others from Ibn Mas'ud agree on the absence of this phrase. The passage then details two groups of narrations: one that separates and identifies the addition, and another that omits it entirely. This analysis demonstrates how differences in sanad (chain) and matn (text) can lead to different fiqh rulings; here, the inclusion or exclusion of this phrase affects the ruling on whether one may stand or sit after the tashahhud before the taslim.

Key Terms
إدراج (idrāj): Insertion of a narrator's own words into the hadith text, making it appear as part of the Prophet's speech.
سند (sanad): The chain of narrators transmitting the hadith.
متن (matn): The text of the hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Dispute over Addition in Tashahhud Hadith
Analyzing variant narrations of tashahhud hadith and their effect on fiqh ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze variant narrations of a hadith chain and evaluate the impact of narrator weakness on the fiqh ruling, specifically regarding the addition in the tashahhud hadith.

Core Idea
A single phrase's authenticity as marfu' or mawquf determines whether standing after tashahhud is sunnah or merely permissible.

The tashahhud hadith is foundational for prayer rulings. However, a phrase 'If you say this or do this, then if you wish, stand up' is disputed: is it part of the prophetic text (marfu') or a companion's statement (mawquf)? This lesson examines the chains of narration, identifies a weak narrator, and clarifies the fiqh consequence according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed phrase
Locate the addition 'إذا قلت هذا أو فعلت هذا فإن شئت فقم' in the tashahhud hadith and note that it is the subject of scholarly debate.
The tashahhud is recited in every sitting of the prayer.
2
List narrators from Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah
The author lists seven narrators who transmitted the hadith from Zuhayr. Recognize that the addition appears in their versions.
These include Ahmad ibn Abdullah ibn Yunus, Abu Dawud al-Tayalisi, etc.
3
Identify the problematic narrator
Muhammad ibn Aban is singled out as the one who used to insert the addition into the marfu' hadith. Compare his narration with others.
Ibn Aban is known as a weak narrator.
4
Evaluate narrator credibility
Ibn Hibban declared Muhammad ibn Aban weak in al-Majruhin. Al-Daraqutni did not note any follow-up (mutaba'ah) for him. This weakens the marfu' claim.
Weak narrator implies the addition is not reliably from the Prophet.
5
Determine fiqh implication
If the addition is mawquf (Ibn Mas'ud's statement), then standing after tashahhud is not a sunnah but merely permissible. The Hanafi school adopts this view.
The Shafi'i school considers it marfu' and thus sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثاً: إن الرُّوَاة عن زهير بن معاوية اختلفوا عليه في رِوَايَة هَذَا الْحَدِيث، فرواه كُلّ من: أحمد بن عبد الله بن يونس اليربوعي، وأبو داود الطيالسي، وعاصم بن علي، وعبد الله بن محمد النفيلي، وعلي بن الجعد، ومالك بن إسماعيل النهدي، وموسى بن داود الضبي. فإذا قلت هذا أو فعلت هذا، فإن شئت فقم. وهذا يدل على أن محمد بن أبان كان ممن يدرج هذه الزيادة في الحديث المرفوع، إلا أن ابن حبان عقب على هذه الرواية بقوله: محمد بن أبان ضعيف قد تبرأنا من عهدته في كتاب المجروحين. ولم يشر الدارقطني في علله إلى متابعة محمد بن أبان. ولعل هذا الخلاف في كون رواية أبان متابعة لابن ثوبان، أو متابعة لزهير هي التي جعلت ابن الصلاح يضرب عن التصريح باسمه، واكتفى بالإشارة إلى وجودها بقوله: وغيرهما.
Thirdly: The narrators from Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah differed regarding the narration of this hadith. So it was narrated by each of: Ahmad ibn Abdullah ibn Yunus al-Yarbu'i, Abu Dawud al-Tayalisi, 'Asim ibn 'Ali, 'Abdullah ibn Muhammad al-Nufayli, 'Ali ibn al-Ja'd, Malik ibn Isma'il al-Nahdi, and Musa ibn Dawud al-Dabbi. [And the addition is] 'If you say this or do this, then if you wish, stand up.' And this indicates that Muhammad ibn Aban was one of those who used to insert this addition into the marfu' hadith, except that Ibn Hibban commented on this narration saying: 'Muhammad ibn Aban is weak, we have disassociated ourselves from his responsibility in the book "al-Majruhin".' And al-Daraqutni did not indicate in his 'Ilal that Muhammad ibn Aban was followed. Perhaps this disagreement over whether the narration of Aban is a follow-up to Ibn Thawban or a follow-up to Zuhayr is what caused Ibn al-Salah to refrain from explicitly naming him, and sufficed with an allusion to its existence by saying: 'and others.'
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the chains for the tashahhud hadith, specifically focusing on the disagreement among narrators from Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah. The author lists seven narrators who transmitted the hadith from Zuhayr, and then notes that the phrase 'If you say this or do this, then if you wish, stand up' is included in their versions. However, the discussion centers on whether this phrase is part of the marfu' (prophetic) text or an insertion by Muhammad ibn Aban. The author notes that Ibn Hibban declared Muhammad ibn Aban weak, and al-Daraqutni did not confirm anyone following him, indicating that the added phrase is likely not authentic as marfu'. Ibn al-Salah alluded to this issue with the phrase 'and others' without naming Muhammad ibn Aban, possibly because of the uncertainty about whether Aban's narration supports Ibn Thawban's version or Zuhayr's. This hadith disagreement impacts the fiqh ruling: if the phrase is marfu', then standing after tashahhud is a sunnah; if it is mawquf (a statement of Ibn Mas'ud), then it is merely permissible. The Hanafi school typically adopts the latter view, considering the phrase as mawquf and thus not a required part of tashahhud.

Key Terms
المرفوع (al-marfu'): A hadith attributed directly to the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him).
الموقوف (al-mawquf): A hadith attributed to a Companion, not to the Prophet.
يدرج (yudrij): To insert or incorporate something into the text of a hadith, often spuriously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Majority View on Tashahhud: Command as Evidence of Obligation
Presents the majority view that tashahhud is obligatory with two hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two hadith proofs for the obligation of tashahhud, explain how the imperative form (amr) indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul, and compare the majority (fard) and Hanafi (wajib) positions.

Core Idea
A command (amr) from the Prophet indicates obligation unless a contextual indicator overrides it.

This lesson examines the majority view that tashahhud is fard (obligatory) in prayer. The source presents two hadith proofs: one from Ibn Mas'ud using the phrase 'before tashahhud was prescribed' and the command 'say at-tahiyyat', and another from Ali defining the boundaries of prayer. Understanding how commands are interpreted in usul al-fiqh is crucial for deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the majority position
The passage states that the majority of jurists hold tashahhud as fard. Note that the Hanafi school says it is wajib, not fard, but that distinction is not in this passage.
The term 'fard' here is used in the general sense of obligatory; Hanafis reserve 'fard' for matters proven by qat'i evidence.
2
Examine the first proof: Ibn Mas'ud hadith
Two sub-arguments: (a) the companion's phrase 'before tashahhud was prescribed' implies it became obligatory; (b) the Prophet's command 'say at-tahiyyat' is an imperative, which in usul indicates obligation.
The phrase 'قبل أن يفرض التشهد' shows the early understanding that tashahhud was prescribed (farada).
3
Apply the usul principle: al-amr yufidu al-wujub
In Hanafi usul, a command from the Prophet indicates wujub unless there is a contextual indicator to the contrary. Here, no such indicator is present.
The majority also accept this principle but do not distinguish between fard and wajib based on the strength of the dalil.
4
Examine the second proof: Hadith of Ali
The hadith states 'its tahlil is taslim' – the closing of prayer is by taslim, implying that what is between takbir and taslim is part of the prayer's obligatory structure.
This hadith is used as supporting evidence (ta'ayud) for the obligation of tashahhud.
5
Compare with the Hanafi view
The Hanafi school differentiates between fard (from qat'i dalil) and wajib (from zanni dalil). They accept the command but argue that tashahhud is established by ahad hadith, making it wajib, not fard.
The practical difference: intentional omission invalidates prayer according to the majority; Hanafis say prayer is valid but requires sajdah al-sahw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن
المذهب الثاني:
ذهب جمهور الفقهاء إلى القول بفرضيتهما). واستدلوا:
١ . بِمَا روي عن عَبْد الله بن مسعود ، قَالَ: كنا نقول قَبْلَ أن يفرض التشهد: السلام عَلَى الله السلام عَلَى جبريل وميكائيل. فَقَالَ رَسُول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: لا تقولوا هكذا فإن الله هُوَ السلام، وَلَكِنْ قولوا التحيات الله ... الْحَدِيث»).
ووجه الدلالة من هَذَا الْحَدِيث أمران:
أ. قوله: «قَبْلَ أن يفرض التشهد فدل ذَلِكَ عَلَى أن التشهد فرض.
ب. قوله «قولوا التحيات أمر، والأمر يقتضي الوجوب.
.٢ استدلوا أَيْضاً بِمَا روي عن علي الله عن النَّبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «مفتاح الصَّلاة الطهور، وتحريمها التكبير، وتحليلها التسليم.
قَالَ التِّرْمِذِي عقب روايته لَهُ: «هَذَا الْحَدِيث أصح شيء في الباب وأحسن».
Chapter Three: Disagreement in Sanad and Matn. The Second Madhhab: The majority of jurists held that both [tashahhud] are obligatory. They adduced evidence: 1. From what is narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, who said: We used to say before tashahhud was prescribed: Peace be upon Allah, peace be upon Jibril and Mika'il. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: Do not say that, for Allah is Peace, but say: At-tahiyyatu lillah... the hadith. The indication from this hadith is in two matters: a. His saying 'before tashahhud was prescribed' indicates that tashahhud is obligatory. b. His saying 'say at-tahiyyat' is a command, and a command necessitates obligation. 2. They also adduced evidence from what is narrated from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'The key to prayer is purification, its tahrîm is takbîr, and its tahlîl is taslîm.' At-Tirmidhi said after narrating it: 'This hadith is the most authentic thing on this topic and the best.'
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion in the disagreement about the obligation of tashahhud in prayer. The majority of jurists (جمهور الفقهاء) hold that tashahhud is obligatory (فرض). They provide two hadith proofs. First, the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, which contains the phrase 'before tashahhud was prescribed' (قبل أن يفرض التشهد) — this shows that the Companions understood tashahhud as something that becomes obligatory. Second, the command 'say at-tahiyyat' is a command from the Prophet, and in usul, a command indicates obligation (الأمر يقتضي الوجوب). The second hadith from Ali defines the boundaries of prayer: takbir enters one into prayer, taslim exits. This hadith is used to support the idea that the components mentioned are essential. It is important to note that the Hanafi madhhab considers tashahhud as wajib (necessary) rather than fard (obligatory), but the text here presents the majority view. The commentator (the author of this work) is presenting the dispute.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): Obligatory act the omission of which invalidates the prayer in the majority view.
تشهد (tashahhud): The testimony recited in the sitting posture of prayer.
أمر (amr): A command; in usul, the imperative form indicates obligation unless there is a contextual indicator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority, what is the ruling of tashahhud in prayer?
A) Sunnah
B) Wajib
C) Fard
D) Mustahab

Key Takeaway
The majority view that tashahhud is fard relies on the usul principle that a command indicates obligation, but the Hanafi school distinguishes fard from wajib based on the certainty of the evidence.

Idraj in the Sanad: First Division of Insertion
Defines the first type of insertion in sanad with the Ibn Mas'ud hadith example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first division of idraj in the sanad and explain how combining different chains without distinction constitutes an insertion, using the example of the Ibn Mas'ud hadith.

Core Idea
Idraj in sanad: a narrator merges multiple chains, hiding discrepancies — this defect (illah) requires careful scrutiny.

Hadith transmission demands precise reporting of chains. When a narrator combines two distinct chains without separating them, they commit idraj (insertion) in the sanad. This lesson focuses on the first division of such idraj, where the text remains unchanged but the chain becomes composite. Understanding this is essential for evaluating hadith reliability, a key skill in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define idraj in sanad
Begin with the definition: idraj is an insertion into the chain, where a narrator adds or merges narrators from different versions without distinction.
Idraj can occur in sanad or matn; here we focus on sanad.
2
Recognize the first division
The first division occurs when the text has multiple chains due to different narrators, and one narrator carries all versions together without separating them.
This is distinct from later divisions where the text may also change.
3
Examine the Ibn Mas'ud example
Identify the two chains: Mansur and al-A'mash narrate via Amr ibn Shurahbil, while Wasil al-Ahdab narrates directly from Abu Wa'il without Amr. Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Muhammad ibn Kathir combined all three in one sanad, causing idraj.
The insertion here is adding Amr ibn Shurahbil into Wasil's chain.
4
See how Yahya al-Qattan clarified
Yahya al-Qattan transmitted the narrations separately, showing the correct distinct chains. This highlights the importance of distinguishing versions.
His version is considered more reliable as it avoids idraj.
5
Connect to usul al-fiqh
Idraj in sanad can weaken a hadith's authenticity. In Hanafi usul, such a hadith requires investigation of each chain before it can be used as dalil.
A hadith with undetected idraj may lead to incorrect legal rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني: أن يقع الإدراج في السند دون الْمَتْن
ويمكن أن نجعل هَذَا النوع على خمسة أقسام:
القسم الأول:
أن يَكُون المَتْن مختلف الإسناد بالنسبة إلى أفراد ،رواته، فيرويه راو واحد عَنْهُمْ، فيحمل بعض رواياتهم على بعض ولا يميز بينها. ومثاله ما رَوَاهُ عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن مهدي ومحمد بن كثير العبدي، عن سفيان الثوري، عن منصور والأعمش وواصل الأحدب ()، عن أبي وائل، عن عمرو بن شرحبيل)، عن ابن مسعود قُلْتُ: (يا رَسُول الله أي الذنب أعظم؟ الحديث).
فَقَدْ أَدرج عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن مهدي ومحمد بن كثير في هَذَا السند، إِذْ إِن منصوراً والأعمش يرويانه عن أبي وائل عن عمرو بن شرحبيل، عن ابن مسعود، أما واصل فيرويه عن أبي وائل، عن ابن مسعود لا يذكر فيه عمرو بن شرحبيل.
وَقَدْ رَوَاهُ عن واصل بن حيان الأسدي الأحدب جَمَاعَة من الرُّوَاةِ مِنْهُمْ: ١ سعيد بن مسروق، ٢ شعبة بن الحجاج، ٣ مالك بن مغول، ٤ مهدي بن ميمون. فَلَمْ يذكروا في روايتهم عن واصل عمرو بن شرحبيل، وإنما عمرو مذكور في رِوَايَة منصور والأعمش. وَقَدْ بين الإسنادين يحيى بن سعيد القطان في روايته، فأخرج:
The second type: Insertion (idraj) in the chain without the text. We can divide this type into five divisions: First division: That the text has different chains relative to its individual narrators, so a single narrator narrates it from them, carrying their narrations together without distinguishing between them. An example is what was narrated by Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Muhammad ibn Kathir al-Abdi, from Sufyan al-Thawri, from Mansur, al-A'mash, and Wasil al-Ahdab, from Abu Wa'il, from Amr ibn Shurahbil, from Ibn Mas'ud: I said, 'O Messenger of Allah, which sin is greatest? ... the hadith.' So Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Muhammad ibn Kathir inserted [the narrator] in this chain, because Mansur and al-A'mash narrate it from Abu Wa'il from Amr ibn Shurahbil from Ibn Mas'ud, while Wasil narrates it from Abu Wa'il from Ibn Mas'ud without mentioning Amr ibn Shurahbil. A group of narrators narrated it from Wasil ibn Hayyan al-Ahdab, including: 1. Sa'id ibn Masruq, 2. Shu'ba ibn al-Hajjaj, 3. Malik ibn Mighwal, 4. Mahdi ibn Maimun. They did not mention Amr ibn Shurahbil in their narration from Wasil; rather, Amr is mentioned in the narration of Mansur and al-A'mash. Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Qattan clarified the two chains in his narration, so he narrated:
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage discusses a technical issue in hadith transmission known as idraj (insertion). Here the insertion occurs in the sanad (chain) rather than the matn (text). The author mentions that this type can be divided into five categories. The first category is when a narrator receives a hadith from multiple chains that differ in some narrators, and then transmits them together without distinguishing the differences. The example given is the narration of Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Muhammad ibn Kathir al-Abdi from Sufyan al-Thawri from a group, combining the chains of Mansur and al-A'mash (which include Amr ibn Shurahbil) with the chain of Wasil (which does not include Amr). This merging without differentiation constitutes idraj in the sanad. The commentators note that other narrators who transmitted from Wasil did not include Amr, confirming the discrepancy. This analysis is crucial for usul al-fiqh because the authenticity and wording of a hadith can be affected by such insertions, impacting the derivation of legal rulings.

Key Terms
إدراج (idraj): Insertion into the chain of narration where a narrator combines different chains without distinction.
سند (sanad): Chain of narrators transmitting the hadith.
متن (matn): Text of the hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first division of idraj in the sanad?
A) Adding a narrator from one chain to another
B) Dropping a narrator from the chain
C) Changing the wording of the matn
D) Combining multiple chains without distinction

Key Takeaway
The first division of idraj in sanad occurs when a narrator combines different chains without distinction, as exemplified by the Ibn Mas'ud hadith transmitted by Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Muhammad ibn Kathir.

Idraj in the Chain: The Ibn Mas'ud Hadith Case Study
Analyzing conflicting chains in a hadith narrated by Ibn Mas'ud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze idraj in the chain of a hadith by comparing narrator transmissions and determining the correct sanad.

Core Idea
Chain idraj inserts a narrator into a chain; critical comparison of multiple riwayat reveals the error.

This passage presents a classic example of idraj occurring not in the text but in the chain of narrators. By examining the narrations of Ibn Mas'ud's hadith through different transmitters, we see how 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Muhammad ibn Kathir mistakenly combined chains, while Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Qattan preserved the correct separation. Understanding such cases sharpens our hadith criticism skills, essential for evaluating the authenticity of prophetic traditions used in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting transmissions
The passage mentions two versions: one via Mansur/al-A'mash including 'Amr ibn Shurahbīl, and one via Wāṣil without him. Determine which narrators transmitted each.
Focus on the narrators: Mansur and al-A'mash vs. Wāṣil.
2
Recognize the idraj
'Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Muhammad ibn Kathir combined them, inserting 'Amr into Wāṣil's chain. This is idraj in the sanad.
The error is combining distinct chains, not inventing a narrator.
3
Analyze the distinguishing narrations
Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Qattan separated them, and his version is supported by Shu'ba and Mahdi ibn Maymun. Note the importance of multiple corroborating transmissions.
Consistency across reliable narrators strengthens the correct version.
4
Evaluate the scholarly judgment
The passage concludes that Yahya's version is correct (al-sawab) based on consistency with other narrators. This illustrates the principle of tarjih (preferring one chain over another) in hadith criticism.
Tarjih here favors the separate chains over the combined one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَقَدْ أَدرج عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن مهدي ومحمد بن كثير في هَذَا السند، إِذْ إِن منصوراً والأعمش يرويانه عن أبي وائل عن عمرو بن شرحبيل، عن ابن مسعود، أما واصل فيرويه عن أبي وائل، عن ابن مسعود لا يذكر فيه عمرو بن شرحبيل. وَقَدْ رَوَاهُ عن واصل بن حيان الأسدي الأحدبِ جَمَاعَة من الرُّوَاةِ مِنْهُمْ: سعيد بن مسروق، شُعْبة بن الحجاج، مالك بن مِغْوَل، مهدي بن ميمون. فَلَمْ يذكروا في روايتهم عن واصل عمرو بن شرحبيل، وإنما عمرو مذكور في رِوَايَة منصور والأعمش. وَقَدْ بين الإسنادين يحيى بن سعيد القطان في روايته، فأخرج: البخاري، والدارقطني، والخطيب من طرق عن يحيى بن سعيد القطان، عن سفيان، قَالَ: حَدَّثَنَا منصور والأعمش، عن أبي وائل عن أبي ميسرة، عن عبد الله. قَالَ سفيان وحدثني واصل عن أبي وائل عن عَبْد الله بِهِ. قَالَ الدَّارَقُطْنِي: «قَالَ لنا أبو بكر النيسابوري: هكذا رَوَاهُ يحيى، وَلَمْ يذكر في حَدِيث واصل عمرو بن شرحبيل ورواه عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن مهدي ومحمد بن كَثِير فجمعا بَيْنَ واصل ومنصور والأعمش، عن أبي وائل عن عمرو بن شرحبيل، عن عبد الله، فيشبه أن يَكُون الثوري جمع بَيْنَ الثلاثة لعبد الرحمن بن مهدي ولابن كثير فجعل إسنادهم واحداً، وَلَمْ يذكر بينهم خلافاً، وحمل حَدِيث واصل عَلَى حَدِيث الأعمش ومنصور، وفصله يحيى بن سعيد فجعل حَدِيث واصل عن أبي وائل عن عَبْد الله - وَهُوَ الصواب؛ لأن شعبة ومهدي بن ميمون روياه عن واصل، عن أبي وائل، عن عبد الله كَمَا رَوَاهُ يَحْيَى عن الثوري عَنْهُ، والله أعلم.
Indeed, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Muhammad ibn Kathir committed idraj in this chain, because Mansur and al-A'mash narrate it from Abu Wa'il from 'Amr ibn Shurahbīl from Ibn Mas'ud, while Wāṣil narrates it from Abu Wa'il from Ibn Mas'ud without mentioning 'Amr ibn Shurahbīl. And it was narrated from Wāṣil ibn Hayyān al-Asadī al-Aḥdab by a group of narrators, among them: Sa'īd ibn Masrūq, Shu'ba ibn al-Ḥajjāj, Mālik ibn Mighwal, Mahdī ibn Maymūn. They did not mention 'Amr ibn Shurahbīl in their narration from Wāṣil; rather 'Amr is mentioned in the narration of Mansur and al-A'mash. And Yaḥyā ibn Sa'īd al-Qaṭṭān clarified the two chains in his narration, and he transmitted: al-Bukhārī, al-Dāraquṭnī, and al-Khaṭīb through multiple paths from Yaḥyā ibn Sa'īd al-Qaṭṭān, from Sufyān, who said: Mansur and al-A'mash narrated to us, from Abu Wa'il from Abu Maysara from 'Abdullāh. Sufyān said: And Wāṣil narrated to me from Abu Wa'il from 'Abdullāh with it. Al-Dāraquṭnī said: Abu Bakr al-Naysābūrī told us: this is how Yaḥyā narrated it, and he did not mention in the hadith of Wāṣil 'Amr ibn Shurahbīl. And 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Muhammad ibn Kathīr narrated it and combined between Wāṣil, Mansur, and al-A'mash, from Abu Wa'il from 'Amr ibn Shurahbīl from 'Abdullāh. It is likely that al-Thawrī combined the three for 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Ibn Kathīr, making their chain one, and did not mention any difference between them, and carried the hadith of Wāṣil to the hadith of al-A'mash and Mansur. But Yaḥyā ibn Sa'īd separated them, making the hadith of Wāṣil from Abu Wa'il from 'Abdullāh — and this is correct, because Shu'ba and Mahdī ibn Maymūn narrated it from Wāṣil, from Abu Wa'il, from 'Abdullāh, just as Yaḥyā narrated from al-Thawrī from him. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage presents a case study of idraj (insertion) in the chain of a hadith. The hadith in question is narrated from Ibn Mas'ud through multiple chains. 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Muhammad ibn Kathir combined the chains of Mansur/al-A'mash (which include 'Amr ibn Shurahbīl) and Wāṣil (which does not), thereby inserting 'Amr into Wāṣil's chain. The correct distinction was made by Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, who kept the chains separate, and his version is supported by other narrators like Shu'ba and Mahdi ibn Maymun. This illustrates how careful attention to chain variations can prevent idraj and preserve the integrity of hadith transmission. The Hanafi school, like all schools, relies on such critical analysis of chains to determine the authenticity and wording of prophetic traditions that form the basis of legal rulings.

Key Terms
إدراج (idrāj): The insertion of foreign material into a hadith, either in the chain or text, by a narrator.
سند (sanad): The chain of narrators transmitting a hadith.
متن (matn): The text or content of a hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, what did 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi and Muhammad ibn Kathir do incorrectly?
A) They omitted 'Amr ibn Shurahbīl from the chain.
B) They combined the chain of Wāṣil with that of Mansur and al-A'mash, inserting 'Amr ibn Shurahbīl into Wāṣil's narration.
C) They narrated the hadith from a weak narrator.
D) They changed the wording of the text.

Key Takeaway
Idraj in the chain is as serious as idraj in the text; careful separation of narrations is essential for hadith reliability.

Second Type of Idraj: Text from Different Chain
Defining and exemplifying idraj where part of the matn comes from a different chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the second type of idraj and identify the inserted part in a hadith example.

Core Idea
In idraj type 2, a narrator appends an extra phrase from a different chain without telling you.

When a narrator has a hadith with one chain, but also knows an additional detail from another chain, he might combine them. This can obscure which part of the text belongs to which source. The second type of idraj occurs when the narrator inserts that extra part into the first chain and presents the text as one unit. Recognizing this is essential for accurate hadith criticism and understanding legal derivations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two chains
In the example, the main chain is 'Āṣim ibn Kulayb from his father from Wā'il ibn Ḥujr. The additional part comes from 'Āṣim ibn Kulayb from 'Abd al-Jabbār ibn Wā'il from some of his family from Wā'il ibn Ḥujr.
The main chain is given first; the second chain is often shorter or less well-known.
2
Locate the inserted part
Find the phrase that comes from the second chain: 'Then I came to them after that in a time of severe cold and I saw them moving their hands from under the clothes.' This is appended to the end of the main hadith.
The inserted part often seems like an additional observation or commentary.
3
Understand the narratorial action
The narrator (e.g., Sufyān ibn 'Uyayna) inserted that phrase into the main narration without mentioning the second chain, creating a combined text.
This is idraj because the extra part is from a different chain and is not flagged.
4
Recognize the effect on fiqh
If the inserted part is taken as part of the main hadith, it could affect rulings; for example, moving hands under clothes might be considered part of the prayer description, whereas it is actually a separate incident.
Hanafi scholars stress distinguishing such insertions to avoid misapplying the Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني: أن يَكُوْن متن الْحَدِيث عِنْدَ الرَّاوِي بإسناد إلا طرفاً منه فإنه عنده بإسناد آخر، فیدرجه من رَوَاهُ عَنْهُ عَلَى الإِسناد الأول ويسوق المثن تاماً، ولا يذكر الإسناد الثاني. مثاله : ما رَوَاهُ سفيان بن عيينة وزائدة بن قدامة عن عاصم بن كليب، عن أبيه، عن وائل بن حجر - وذكر حَدِيث صفة صلاة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم - وفي آخره: «ثم جئتهم بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ في زمان فيْهِ برد شديد فرأيتهم يحركون أيديهم من تحت الثياب). فقوله: «ثُمَّ جئتهم بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ ...» من رِوَايَة عاصم بن كليب، عن عبد الجبار بن وائل عن بعض أهله عن وائل بن حجر، وممن رَوَاهُ عَلَى هَذِهِ الشاكلة فميز بَيْنَ
The second type: That the text of the hadith is with the narrator from one chain except for a part of it which is with him from another chain, so the one who narrates it from him inserts it into the first chain and presents the complete text, and does not mention the second chain. Example: What Sufyān ibn 'Uyayna and Zā'ida ibn Qudāma narrated from 'Āṣim ibn Kulayb, from his father, from Wā'il ibn Ḥujr — and he mentioned the hadith describing the prayer of the Prophet (peace be upon him) — and at its end: 'Then I came to them after that in a time of severe cold and I saw them moving their hands from under the clothes.' So his statement: 'Then I came to them after that...' is from the narration of 'Āṣim ibn Kulayb, from 'Abd al-Jabbār ibn Wā'il, from some of his family, from Wā'il ibn Ḥujr. And among those who narrated it in this manner and distinguished between...
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage defines the second category of idraj (insertion) in hadith. Here, the narrator has the main part of the hadith with one chain, but an additional phrase (often an explanatory note or an observation) comes from a different chain. The narrator combines them by appending the extra phrase to the end of the main hadith without indicating the separate chain. The example given is the famous hadith of Wā'il ibn Ḥujr describing the Prophet's prayer. The core of the hadith is from 'Āṣim ibn Kulayb from his father from Wā'il, but the final observation about seeing people move their hands under their clothes in cold weather comes from 'Āṣim ibn Kulayb from 'Abd al-Jabbār ibn Wā'il from some family members. Sufyān ibn 'Uyayna and Zā'ida ibn Qudāma narrated the combined version, but other narrators correctly separated the chains. This type of idraj is significant because it can affect the legal ruling derived from the hadith; for instance, the hand movement under clothes might be seen as part of the prayer description, but it is actually a separate incident. Hanafi scholars, like all scholars of hadith, stress the importance of distinguishing such insertions to ensure accurate transmission of prophetic practice.

Key Terms
إدراج (idrāj): The insertion of foreign material into a hadith, either in the chain or text, by a narrator.
متن (matn): The text or content of a hadith.
طرف (ṭaraf): A part or portion of the hadith text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second type of idraj, what does the narrator do with the part of the hadith that comes from a different chain?
A) He narrates it separately with its own chain.
B) He inserts it into the first chain and presents the complete text without mentioning the second chain.
C) He omits that part entirely.
D) He changes the wording to match the first chain.

Key Takeaway
The second type of idraj is when a narrator appends a portion from a different chain to the main text without indicating the separate chain, potentially altering the legal understanding.

Second Type of Idraj: Inserting a Segment from a Different Sanad
Defining the second type of idraj with a detailed hadith example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second type of idraj (insertion of a hadith segment from a different chain) and apply the method of comparing narrators to detect such insertions.

Core Idea
The second type of idraj merges two chains into one — careful comparison reveals the error.

This passage introduces the second type of idraj (insertion) in hadith criticism. Unlike the first type where a narrator's own words are inserted, here a segment from a completely different chain of transmission is attached to the main hadith. The example of the prayer description hadith shows how scholars like Musa ibn Harun al-Hammal used the fact that the majority of narrators did not include the extra part to determine that it was an insertion. Understanding this type of idraj is crucial for correctly attributing hadith texts and deriving legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Define the second type of idraj
The type where a narrator has a hadith with one chain except a part that is with another chain, and they narrate the whole as if from the first chain.
This is different from the first type where a narrator inserts their own words.
2
Identify the two chains in the example
Main chain: Sufyan ibn 'Uyaynah and Za'idah ibn Qudamah from 'Asim ibn Kulayb from his father from Wa'il ibn Hujr. Inserted part chain: 'Asim from 'Abd al-Jabbar from his family from Wa'il.
The inserted part begins with 'Then I came to them after that...'
3
Note which narrators distinguished the parts
Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah and Shuja' ibn al-Walid narrated the hadith with the two parts separated and each given its proper chain.
Their versions are found in Ahmad, al-Tabarani, and al-Khatib.
4
Observe the majority of narrators
Eleven reliable narrators (Sufyan al-Thawri, Shu'bah, etc.) narrated the hadith from 'Asim without the inserted part, strengthening the identification of idraj.
Their agreement makes the insertion unlikely to be original.
5
Understand the scholarly judgment
Al-Hafiz Musa ibn Harun al-Hammal pronounces the combined version as a mistake (wahm) and declares Zuhayr and Shuja' more reliable.
The correct version is the one where the chains are kept separate.

القسم الثاني: أن يكون متن الحديث عند الراوي بإسناد إلا طرفاً منه فإنه عنده بإسناد آخر، فيدرجه من رواه عنه على الإسناد الأول ويسوق المتن تاماً، ولا يذكر الإسناد الثاني. مثاله: ما رواه سفيان بن عيينة وزائدة بن قدامة عن عاصم بن كليب، عن أبيه، عن وائل بن حجر – وذكر حديث صفة صلاة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم – وفي آخره: «ثم جئتهم بعد ذلك في زمان فيه برد شديد فرأيتهم يحركون أيديهم من تحت الثياب». فقوله: «ثم جئتهم بعد ذلك ...» من رواية عاصم بن كليب، عن عبد الجبار بن وائل عن بعض أهله عن وائل بن حجر، وممن رواه على هذه الشاكلة فميز بين جزأي المتن: ١. زهير بن معاوية وروايته عند: أحمد، والطبراني، والخطيب. ٢. شجاع بن الوليد: عند الخطيب. ومما يقوي الحكم بالإدراج في إسناد هذا الحديث أن أحد عشر راوياً وهم: سفيان الثوري وشعبة، وأبو الأحوص، وأبو عوانة، وخالد بن عبد الله، وصالح بن عمر، وعبد الواحد بن زياد وجرير بن عبد الحميد، وبشر بن المفضل، وعبيدة بن حميد، وعبد العزيز بن مسلم، رووا هذا الحديث عن عاصم ولم يتطرقوا إلى ذكر هذا الإدراج. قال الحافظ موسى بن هارون الحمال: وذلك – يعني رواية سفيان وزائدة – عندنا وهم، وإنما أدرج عليه، وهو من رواية عاصم، عن عبد الجبار بن وائل، عن أهله، عن وائل، هكذا رواه مبيناً زهير بن معاوية وأبو بدر شجاع بن الوليد، فميزا قصة تحريك الأيدي من تحت الثياب وفصلاها من الحديث وذكرا إسنادهما كما ذكرنا. ثم قال: وهذه رواية مضبوطة، اتفق عليه زهير وشجاع بن الوليد، وهما أثبت له رواية ممن روى رفع الأيدي من تحت الثياب عن عاصم بن كليب، عن أبيه، عن وائل.
The second division: That the text of the hadith is with the narrator through one chain of transmission except for a part of it which is with him through another chain, so the one who narrates it from him inserts it into the first chain and presents the full text without mentioning the second chain. Its example: What Sufyan ibn 'Uyaynah and Za'idah ibn Qudamah narrated from 'Asim ibn Kulayb, from his father, from Wa'il ibn Hujr – and he mentioned the hadith describing the prayer of the Prophet (peace be upon him) – and at its end: 'Then I came to them after that in a time of severe cold and I saw them moving their hands from under the garments.' So his statement: 'Then I came to them after that...' is from the narration of 'Asim ibn Kulayb, from 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn Wa'il, from some of his family, from Wa'il ibn Hujr. And among those who narrated it in this manner and distinguished between the two parts of the text are: 1. Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah – his narration is with Ahmad, al-Tabarani, and al-Khatib. 2. Shuja' ibn al-Walid – with al-Khatib. Among what strengthens the judgment of insertion in the chain of this hadith is that eleven narrators, namely: Sufyan al-Thawri, Shu'bah, Abu al-Ahwas, Abu 'Awanah, Khalid ibn 'Abd Allah, Salih ibn 'Umar, 'Abd al-Wahid ibn Ziyad, Jarir ibn 'Abd al-Hamid, Bishr ibn al-Mufaddal, 'Ubaydah ibn Humayd, and 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn Muslim, narrated this hadith from 'Asim and did not mention this insertion. Al-Hafiz Musa ibn Harun al-Hammal said: 'That – meaning the narration of Sufyan and Za'idah – in our view is a mistake; it was only inserted upon him, and it is from the narration of 'Asim, from 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn Wa'il, from his family, from Wa'il. This is how Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah and Abu Badr Shuja' ibn al-Wald clearly narrated it – they distinguished the story of moving the hands from under the garments and separated it from the hadith and mentioned its chain as we mentioned.' Then he said: 'This is a well-recorded narration, agreed upon by Zuhayr and Shuja' ibn al-Walid, and they are more reliable in his narration than those who narrated raising the hands from under the garments from 'Asim ibn Kulayb, from his father, from Wa'il.'
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of idraj (insertion) in hadith: when a narrator has a complete hadith with one sanad, but a portion of the matn comes from a different sanad. The narrator (or the one who reports from him) inserts that portion into the first sanad and presents the whole text as if it all belongs to that one sanad. The example is a well-known hadith about the Prophet's prayer narrated by 'Asim ibn Kulayb. The narrators Sufyan ibn 'Uyaynah and Za'idah ibn Qudamah included at the end a story about moving hands under garments in cold weather. However, this part actually comes from a different chain: 'Asim ibn Kulayb from 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn Wa'il from his family from Wa'il. The scholars identify that some narrators (Zuhayr and Shuja') correctly separated the two parts and gave each its proper chain. The majority of reliable narrators (11 mentioned) narrated the hadith from 'Asim without this addition, confirming that the added part is an idraj. Al-Hafiz Musa ibn Harun al-Hammal pronounces that the combined version is a mistake, and that Zuhayr and Shuja' are more reliable in this case. This passage is a lesson in hadith criticism, illustrating how scholars detect and evaluate insertions.

Key Terms
إدراج (idrāj): Insertion of a part from a different sanad into a hadith matn, merging them without distinguishing the chains.
متن (matn): The text/content of a hadith.
إسناد (isnād): The chain of narrators transmitting a hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the second type of idraj, which part of the hadith was inserted from a different chain?
A) The entire description of the Prophet's prayer.
B) The phrase about severe cold and moving hands under garments.
C) The chain from Wa'il ibn Hujr.
D) The narration of Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah.

Key Takeaway
The second type of idraj involves inserting a segment from a different chain into a hadith; detection relies on comparing multiple narrations and identifying which narrators correctly separated the chains.

The Third Type of Idraj: Interpolation Between Two Different Isnads
Defining the third type of idraj with an example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third type of idraj by analyzing the example of the hadith from Malik and distinguish it from other types of interpolation.

Core Idea
Interpolation between different chains: a narrator inserts a phrase from one hadith into another, even though it is not part of his own narration.

This lesson examines a specific type of hadith interpolation (idraj) where a narrator mixes texts from two different chains. The key example involves Sa'id ibn Abi Maryam adding the phrase 'wa la tanafasu' to a hadith of Anas, a phrase that actually belongs to a hadith from Abu Hurayra. Understanding such interpolation is crucial for verifying the authenticity of hadith used in legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Define the third type of idraj
From the passage: 'That the two texts have different chains, and some narrators interpolate something from one into the other, and that thing is not from the narration of that narrator.' State this definition in your own words.
Contrast with other types where the interpolation comes from the narrator's own words or from the same chain.
2
Examine the example provided
The hadith from Anas via Malik: 'Do not hate each other, do not envy each other, do not turn your backs on each other, and do not compete with each other…' The narrator Sa'id ibn Abi Maryam added 'wa la tanafasu' (do not compete). Identify the narrator and the added phrase.
The added phrase is the key element suspected of interpolation.
3
Identify the source of interpolation
The phrase 'wa la tanafasu' is found in another hadith from Abu Hurayra: 'Do not spy, do not eavesdrop, do not compete with each other, and do not envy each other.' Recognize that Sa'id inserted this phrase from the Abu Hurayra hadith into the Anas hadith.
This is not a case of a narrator adding his own words, but mixing texts from different chains.
4
Recognize the critical method used
Al-Hafiz Hamza al-Kinani identified the interpolation by noting that no other narrator of Malik's hadith included 'wa la tanafasu'. The reliable narrators of al-Muwatta' all omitted it. Understand the importance of comparing multiple chains (turuq) to detect idraj.
This method is essential for hadith criticism and affects which version is considered authentic.
5
Apply to your own analysis
When studying a hadith for fiqh, check if any narrator alone adds a phrase. If so, investigate whether that phrase appears in other hadiths from other chains. This helps determine whether the addition is genuine or an interpolation.
Hanafi scholars rely on such analysis to select the most authentic version for legal ruling.

أن يكون المتنان مختلفي الإسناد، فيدرج بعض الرُّوَاة شَيْئاً من أحدهما في الآخر ولا يَكُون ذَلِكَ الشيء من رِوَايَة ذَلِكَ الرَّاوِي.

Two parts of the text: Chapter Three / Difference in Isnad and Matn. (†) 1. Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiya and his narration according to Ahmad, al-Tabarani, and al-Khatib. 2. Shuja' ibn al-Walid: according to al-Khatib. And what strengthens the judgment of idraj in the chain of this hadith is that eleven narrators: Sufyan al-Thawri, Shu'ba, Abu al-Ahwas, Abu 'Awana, Khalid ibn 'Abdullah, Salih ibn 'Umar, 'Abd al-Wahid ibn Ziyad, Jarir ibn 'Abd al-Hamid, Bishr ibn al-Mufaddal, 'Ubayda ibn Humayd, and 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn Muslim, narrated this hadith from 'Asim and did not mention this idraj. It is from the narration of 'Asim from 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn Wa'il from his family from Wa'il. This is how Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiya and Abu Badr Shuja' ibn al-Walid narrated it clearly, distinguishing the story of moving the hands under the garments and separating it from the hadith, and mentioning their chains as we mentioned." (8) Section Three: That the two texts have different chains, and some narrators interpolate something from one into the other, and that thing is not from the narration of that narrator. Example: What Abu Muhammad Sa'id ibn Abi Maryam al-Hakam ibn Muhammad al-Misri narrated from Malik from al-Zuhri from Anas that the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) said: "Do not hate each other, do not envy each other, do not turn your backs on each other, and do not compete with each other, and be servants of Allah as brothers..." the hadith. It was narrated through this path by al-Khatib and Ibn 'Abd al-Barr. Al-Hafiz Hamza ibn Muhammad al-Kinani said: "I do not know anyone who said in this hadith from Malik: 'and do not compete with each other' except Sa'id ibn Abi Maryam." So Sa'id interpolated the phrase "and do not compete with each other" from the text of another hadith narrated by Malik from Abu al-Zinad from al-A'raj from Abu Hurayra marfu'an: "Beware of suspicion, for suspicion is the most lying of speech. Do not spy, do not eavesdrop, do not compete with each other, and do not envy each other." Both hadiths are correct according to all narrators of al-Muwatta', including: 1. Ahmad ibn Abi Bakr: according to Ibn Hibban. 2. Ishaq ibn 'Isa al-Tabba': according to Ahmad. 3. Isma'il ibn Abi Uways: according to al-Bukhari in al-Adab al-Mufrad.

Commentary
This passage from the book 'The Impact of Differences in Chains and Texts on Differences Among Jurists' discusses a specific type of hadith interpolation (idraj). The author first presents two examples from the earlier section (Zuhayr and Shuja') to illustrate how some narrators inserted a phrase about moving hands under garments into the hadith of Wa'il from 'Asim. The majority of narrators did not include this phrase, indicating that it was interpolated. Then the author defines the third type of idraj: when two hadiths have different chains, and a narrator mixes a phrase from one hadith into the other, even though that phrase is not from his own narration. The example given is the hadith from Anas via Malik, where Sa'id ibn Abi Maryam added the words 'wa la tanafasu' (do not compete with each other). This phrase actually belongs to another hadith from Abu Hurayra. The correct version of the Anas hadith, as narrated by the majority of al-Muwatta' transmitters, does not include 'wa la tanafasu'. Sa'id's addition is considered idraj because he inserted a phrase from a different hadith into this one. The passage shows the meticulousness of hadith critics in identifying such interpolations, which is crucial for accurate legal deduction.

Key Terms
إدراج (idraj): The interpolation of a word or phrase into a hadith by a narrator, either from his own speech or from another hadith, such that it appears to be part of the original hadith.

Idraj in Hadith: The Case of Sa'id ibn Abi Maryam
Textual analysis: detecting hadith mixing through mutaba'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a case of idraj (insertion) in a hadith text and explain how mutaba'ah helps detect such errors in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
A narrator's mistake can insert one hadith's text into another's chain—only a follow-up narration (mutaba'ah) catches it.

This passage presents a concrete example of idraj—where a trustworthy narrator, Sa'id ibn Abi Maryam, accidentally mixed two hadiths, inserting a phrase from one into the other. The author demonstrates the mechanism of mutaba'ah to reveal the error. Understanding such textual corruptions is essential for correct istinbat in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed idraj
The passage states that the two hadiths mixed upon Sa'id ibn Abi Maryam; note that he inserted a phrase from the second hadith into the first.
Idraj can occur in the matn, not only the sanad.
2
List the mutaba'ah narrators
The text lists four groups of narrators who transmitted the correct version from Malik: Sufyan ibn 'Uyayna, Ibn Abi Dhi'b, Zam'a; Shu'ayb ibn Abi Hamza; Muhammad ibn al-Walid al-Zubaydi; Ma'mar ibn Rashid.
These are follow-up narrations that support Malik's hadith.
3
Compare correct vs. mixed version
The mutaba'ah shows the original text; the mixed version has an extra phrase from the second hadith. The correct version is the one consistent across multiple chains.
4
Explain the impact on istinbat
In Hanafi usul, an idraj can change the legal ruling if the inserted text is taken as part of the original hadith. Detecting it prevents incorrect derivation.
Careful textual analysis is a prerequisite for reliable istinbat.
5
Formulate the Hanafi method to verify
Cross-referencing multiple chains (turuq) is the standard method. Mutaba'ah provides corroboration; when a single chain conflicts, the majority consistent version is preferred.
This is a practical application of the rule that zanni evidence is strengthened by multiple routes.

Commentary
This passage analyzes a specific case of idraj (insertion) where the narrator Sa'id ibn Abi Maryam confused two hadiths. The author first lists several narrators who transmitted a particular hadith in a complete mutaba'ah (follow-up) from Malik, showing the correct version. Then it reveals that Sa'id ibn Abi Maryam mistakenly inserted a part from the second hadith into the first, using the chain of the first hadith. This is a key example of how a subtle change in the text (matn) due to mixing can occur, which may affect the legal ruling derived from the hadith.

Key Terms
متابعة (mutaba'ah): A follow-up narration that supports the same hadith from a different chain.
إدراج (idraj): Insertion of extraneous material into a hadith text or chain.

6 min
Fourth Type of Idraj in Hadith
Defines a type of idraj where part not heard directly

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of the fourth type of idraj and distinguish it from the second type.

Core Idea
The fourth type of idraj: same shaykh, but a part heard via intermediary is inserted without mention.

Hadith scholars meticulously classify defects in narrations. One such defect is idraj, where extraneous material is inserted into a hadith. The fourth type involves a scenario where the narrator missed a part from his shaykh but later obtained it through an intermediary and inserted it without specification. Recognizing this type helps in detecting hidden insertions that could affect legal rulings derived from hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
A narrator has a hadith from his shaykh except for a part he did not hear directly.
2
Note the source of the missing part
He hears that missing part from an intermediary between himself and his shaykh.
Intermediary is a student or other link to the same shaykh.
3
Recognize the insertion
The narrator inserts that part into the hadith without specifying its source.
This creates a mixed text.
4
Compare with the second type
In the second type, the inserted part comes from a different shaykh; here the shaykh is the same, but the narrator used an intermediary.
This distinction is crucial for detecting the type of idraj.
5
Understand the consequence
The hadith becomes mixed; detection requires comparing variant narrations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع:
أن يَكُون الْمَتْن عِنْدَ راءٍ إلا جزءاً مِنْهُ، فإنه لَمْ يسمعه من شيخه فيه، وإنما سمعه من واسطة بينه وبين شيخه، فيدرج الرُّوَاة الجزء من الحَدِيث من غَيْر تفصيل (۱۹).
(۱۹) الفرق بينه وبين النوع الثاني أن الطرف المدرج في النوع الثاني هُوَ عن شيخ مغاير لشيخه في بقية المَتْن، وهنا فإن شيخه في كليهما واحد.
Fourth Type: That the hadith is with a narrator except for a part of it, for he did not hear it from his shaykh in it, but rather heard it from an intermediary between him and his shaykh, so the narrator inserts the part of the hadith without specification. (19) The difference between this and the second type is that the inserted part in the second type is from a different shaykh than his shaykh in the rest of the hadith, while here, his shaykh for both is the same.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of idraj (insertion) in hadith. In this type, a narrator has a hadith from his shaykh but missed a part of it. He later hears that missing part from an intermediary between himself and that same shaykh, and then inserts it into the original hadith without specifying the source. The footnote clarifies how this differs from the second type: in the second type, the inserted part comes from a different shaykh altogether, whereas here the shaykh is the same, but the narrator used an intermediary for the inserted part. This classification is important in hadith criticism to detect hidden intrusions.

Key Terms
إدراج (idraj): The insertion of extraneous material into a hadith, whether in the text or chain.
واسطة (wasita): An intermediary narrator between the student and his original shaykh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the fourth type of idraj from the second type?
A) In the fourth type, the inserted part is from a different shaykh
B) In the fourth type, the shaykh for both parts is the same
C) In the fourth type, the narrator heard the whole hadith directly
D) The fourth type is not considered a defect

Key Takeaway
The fourth type of idraj involves a missing part obtained through an intermediary but from the same shaykh, distinguishing it from other types.

Idraj in Hadith: The 'Urayniyyun Narration
Case study of idraj in a hadith chain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify idraj in a hadith and explain how it affects the reliability of the narration in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
A single inserted word from a different chain can change the perceived authenticity of a hadith.

This lesson examines a classic example of idraj (insertion) where narrator Isma'il ibn Ja'far merged two separate chains into one text. Understanding such subtle errors is crucial in usul al-fiqh for evaluating the strength of a hadith used as a legal proof. We will walk through the analysis as performed by hadith critics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the narrated text and the inserted word
Read the hadith text: 'Drink from their milk and urine.' The word 'and their urine' is the point of insertion.
2
Trace the chains for each part
The main part (milk) comes from Humaid directly from Anas. The word 'urine' comes from Humaid via Qatada from Anas.
3
Recognize the insertion (idraj)
Isma'il ibn Ja'far narrated the entire text as if Humaid heard both parts directly from Anas, thus inserting the Qatada portion into the first chain.
4
Compare with correct narration
Other narrators separated the two chains: they narrated the first part from Humaid directly, and then clearly attributed the second part to Qatada.
The clearest narration is from Yazid ibn Harun: 'Humaid said: Qatada said: "and their urine", I did not hear it myself.'
5
Determine the usul implication
This idraj does not invalidate the word 'urine' but shows a flaw in Isma'il's narration. In Hanafi usul, such a hadith is considered 'mudraj' and may affect its ranking if the insertion causes confusion about the chain's continuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن
مثاله: الْحَدِيْثِ الَّذِي رَوَاهُ إسماعيل بن جعفر بن أبي كثير، عن حميد الطويل، عن أنس في قصة العرنيين، وأن رَسُول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ لَهُمْ: «لَوْ خرجتم إلى إبلنا فشربتم من ألبانها وأبوالها».
فلفظه: «وأبوالها» لَمْ يسمعها حميد من أنس مباشرة، وإنما سمعها من قتادة، عن أنس، فأدرجها إسماعيل في الْمَتْن الأول بإسناد الْحَدِيث الأول من غير تفصيل، قَالَ الحافظ الخطيب البغدادي: هكذا رَوَى إسماعيل بن جعفر بن أبي كثير الأنصاري هَذَا الْحَدِيث عن حميد الطويل، عن أنس بن مالك، وفيه لفظة واحدة لم يسمعها حميد عن أنس، وإنما رواها عن قتادة عن أنس، وَهِيَ قوله: «وأبوالها». وَقَدْ رَوَى هَذَا الْحَدِيث عَلَى الصواب ففصل روَايَة قتادة عدة رواة من أصحاب حميد، مِنْهُمْ: ابن أبي عدي، بشر بن المفضل، خالد بن الحارث، عبد الله بن بكر السهمي، مروان بن معاوية الفزاري، معتمر بن سليمان، يزيد بن هارون.
قَالَ الحافظ ابن حجر: كلهم يقول فيه: «فشربتم من ألبانها» قَالَ حميد: قَالَ قتادة عن أنس – رضي الله تَعَالَى عَنْهُ -: «وأبوالها» فرواية إسماعيل عَلَى هَذَا فِيْهَا إدراج وتسوية.
وأصرح الروايات في هَذَا رِوَايَة أبي عوانة من طريق يزيد بن هارون، عن حميد، وفيه: «قَالَ حميد: قَالَ قتادة: «وأبوالها»، لم أسمعه أنا هكذا مثل الخطيب البغدادي وابن حجر لهذا النوع بهذا المثل، واستدرك بعضهم بأن إسماعيل بن جعفر متابع تابعه: عبد الوهاب بن عَبْد المجيد الثقفي: كَمَا عِنْدَ ابن ماجه.
Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text
Example: The hadith that Isma'il ibn Ja'far ibn Abi Kathir narrated from Humaid at-Tawil from Anas regarding the story of the 'Urayniyyun, and that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said to them: 'If you go out to our camels, drink from their milk and urine.' So the word 'and their urine' — Humaid did not hear it directly from Anas, but rather he heard it from Qatada from Anas. So Isma'il inserted it into the first text with the chain of the first hadith without distinction. Al-Hafiz al-Khatib al-Baghdadi said: Thus Isma'il ibn Ja'far ibn Abi Kathir al-Ansari narrated this hadith from Humaid at-Tawil from Anas ibn Malik, and in it is one word that Humaid did not hear from Anas, but rather he narrated it from Qatada from Anas, and it is his saying: 'and their urine.' And several narrators from among the companions of Humaid narrated this hadith correctly, separating the narration of Qatada. Among them: Ibn Abi 'Adi, Bishr ibn al-Mufaddal, Khalid ibn al-Harith, 'Abdullah ibn Bakr as-Sahmi, Marwan ibn Mu'awiya al-Fazari, Mu'tamir ibn Sulayman, Yazid ibn Harun. Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said: All of them say in it: 'Drink from their milk' and Humaid said: Qatada said from Anas — may Allah be pleased with him — 'and their urine'. So Isma'il's narration in this regard contains idraj (insertion) and equalization. And the clearest of the narrations in this is the narration of Abu 'Awana via Yazid ibn Harun from Humaid, in which it says: 'Humaid said: Qatada said: "and their urine", I did not hear it myself like that.' Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi and Ibn Hajar gave this type with this example. And some researchers objected that Isma'il ibn Ja'far has a supporter who followed him: 'Abdul-Wahhab ibn 'Abdul-Majid ath-Thaqafi, as in Ibn Majah.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a case study from the chapter on differences in chains and texts. It presents a well-known hadith about the 'Urayniyyun who were allowed to drink camel milk and urine. The issue is that narrator Isma'il ibn Ja'far combined two separate chains into one text: the main part (milk) from Humaid directly from Anas, but the word 'and their urine' was actually from Humaid via Qatada from Anas. Isma'il 'inserted' (adraja) that word into the first chain as if Humaid heard both parts directly. The correct practice, as shown by other reliable narrators, is to separate the two chains: Humaid would say the first part (milk) and then clearly attribute the word 'urine' to Qatada. This is important in usul al-fiqh because it affects the authenticity of a narration used for legal rulings. The Hanafi madhhab often relies on such hadith for matters like purity and medicine. The passage also highlights how scholars like Ibn Hajar classified this as 'idraj' (insertion), and notes that some argued Isma'il had a supporter, but the predominant view is that his narration contains an error.

Key Terms
الإدراج (al-idraj): Insertion of a word from one chain into another chain's text, blurring the distinction between the two sources.
السند (al-sanad): The chain of narrators transmitting the hadith.
المتن (al-matn): The text of the hadith (the words of the Prophet).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Isma'il ibn Ja'far's narration contain idraj?
A) He invented the word 'urine' himself.
B) He omitted the chain of Qatada and merged his wording into the first chain.
C) He narrated from a weak narrator.
D) He reversed the order of the words.

Key Takeaway
A hadith with idraj is not automatically rejected, but its chain is considered flawed, requiring preference for clearer narrations.

Analysis of the Urayni Hadith: Idraj and Its Impact on Legal Rulings
Detailed examination of variant narrations of the Urayni hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify idraj in a hadith narration and evaluate its impact on deriving a Hanafi ruling.

Core Idea
The addition 'and their urine' is idraj from Qatada, not prophetic — the Hanafi ruling on muharaba does not rely on it.

In this lesson, we examine a famous hadith about the Urayni tribe, which is used to discuss punishments and permissibility of using impure substances for treatment. The key issue is whether the phrase 'and their urine' is part of the prophetic speech or an insertion (idraj) by a narrator. By analyzing the chains and the scholars' assessments, we learn how careful textual criticism directly influences fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the narrators and the disputed wording
List the narrators who transmitted the hadith with the wording 'drink from their milk' and note that some added 'and their urine'. Identify the claim that Isma'il bin Ja'far's narration contains this addition.
The addition is the focus of the analysis.
2
Understand the concept of idraj
Define idraj as the insertion of a narrator's own words into the hadith text, mistakenly believed to be prophetic. Recognize that Ibn Hajar and al-Khatib identified this as idraj from Qatada.
Idraj affects the authenticity of the proof (dalil).
3
Evaluate the claimed mutaba'at (followers)
Examine the three claimed followers of Isma'il: Abdullah bin Umar (weak), Hushaim (combined teachers), and Abdul Wahhab. Assess their strength relative to the seven narrators who did not include the addition.
The majority of reliable narrators support the absence of the addition.
4
Apply the stronger conclusion to usul
Conclude that the addition is idraj, so the hadith does not prove the permissibility of consuming najis. The Hanafi ruling on muharaba relies on other evidence, such as the Quranic verse on retaliation.
This demonstrates how hadith criticism functions in usul al-fiqh.

So there remains only the narration of Abdul Wahhab, and its case can be carried on two probabilities: first: although he followed Isma'il bin Ja'far in it, each of them is not strong enough to resist the opposition of the companions of Humaid, who are seven persons. This is the stronger probability. Second: that it is authentic, so the burden then falls on Humaid, as if he used to clarify the matter to some narrators and keep it ambiguous for others. And Allah knows best.

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed textual analysis of a famous hadith about the Urayni tribe (related to punishments and medical treatment). The author lists the narrators who transmitted the hadith with the wording 'drink from their milk' and then adds the phrase 'and their urine.' Ibn Hajar points out that this addition is inserted (idraj) by Qatada, not part of the prophetic text. The author then critically examines claims that Isma'il bin Ja'far had supporters (mutaba'at) for this addition. He evaluates each claimed follower: Abdullah bin Umar is declared weak by many scholars, and Hushaim's narration is problematic because he combined three teachers. The only remaining follower is Abdul Wahhab, but the author concludes that the majority of Humaid's students (seven) did not include the addition, so the stronger position is that the addition is inserted. This analysis is crucial for Hanafi fiqh, as this hadith is used in discussing the punishment for highway robbers (muharaba) and the permissibility of using impure substances for treatment. The passage exemplifies how careful hadith criticism directly impacts legal rulings.

Key Terms
إدراج (idraj): Insertion of a narrator's own words into the hadith text, mistakenly thought to be prophetic.
تسوية (taswiyah): Smoothing over differences in narration to make them uniform.
متابعة (mutaba'ah): A following narration that supports a specific wording or addition.
